font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Countries where <i>A. thaliana</i> is not found</span></li></ul></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: center; background-color: rgb(180,250,180)"><a href="/wiki/Synonym_(taxonomy)" title="Synonym (taxonomy)">Synonyms</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warwick_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warwick-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align: left">\n<p><i>Arabis thaliana</i>\n</p>\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>Arabidopsis thaliana</b></i>, the <b>thale cress</b>, <b>mouse-ear cress</b> or <b>arabidopsis</b>, is a small <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plant</a> native to <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grin_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-grin-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kramer-natural-history_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-kramer-natural-history-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid28473417_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid28473417-7">[7]</a></sup> <i>A. thaliana</i> is considered a weed; it is found by roadsides and in disturbed land.\n</p><p>A winter <a href="/wiki/Annual_plant" title="Annual plant">annual</a> with a relatively short life cycle, <i>A. thaliana</i> is a popular <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organism</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_biology" title="Plant biology">plant biology</a> and genetics. For a complex multicellular <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryote</a>, <i>A. thaliana</i> has a relatively small <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> of approximately 135 <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">megabase pairs</a> (Mbp).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genome_Assembly_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Genome_Assembly-8">[8]</a></sup> It was the first plant to have its genome sequenced, and is a popular tool for understanding the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a> of many plant traits, including <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flower</a> development and <a href="/wiki/Phototropism" title="Phototropism">light sensing</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" tocsection-25"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:194_Arabidopsis_thaliana,_Turritis_glabra.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="831" data-file-width="442" decoding="async" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/194_Arabidopsis_thaliana%2C_Turritis_glabra.jpg/170px-194_Arabidopsis_thaliana%2C_Turritis_glabra.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/194_Arabidopsis_thaliana%2C_Turritis_glabra.jpg/255px-194_Arabidopsis_thaliana%2C_Turritis_glabra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/194_Arabidopsis_thaliana%2C_Turritis_glabra.jpg/340px-194_Arabidopsis_thaliana%2C_Turritis_glabra.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:194_Arabidopsis_thaliana,_Turritis_glabra.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Botanical illustration</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> is an <a href="/wiki/Annual_plant" title="Annual plant">annual</a> (rarely <a href="/wiki/Biennial_plant" title="Biennial plant">biennial</a>) plant, usually growing to 20&#8211;25&#160;cm tall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kramer-natural-history_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-kramer-natural-history-6">[6]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">leaves</a> form a rosette at the base of the plant, with a few leaves also on the flowering stem. The basal leaves are green to slightly purplish in color, 1.5&#8211;5&#160;cm long and 2&#8211;10&#160;mm broad, with an entire to coarsely serrated margin; the stem leaves are smaller and unstalked, usually with an entire margin. Leaves are covered with small, unicellular hairs (called <a href="/wiki/Trichome" title="Trichome">trichomes</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flowers</a> are 3&#160;mm in diameter, arranged in a <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence#Simple_inflorescences" title="Inflorescence">corymb</a>; their structure is that of the typical <a href="/wiki/Brassicaceae" title="Brassicaceae">Brassicaceae</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siliqua_(plant)" title="Siliqua (plant)">siliqua</a> 5&#8211;20&#160;mm long, containing 20&#8211;30 <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seeds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fnwe_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-fnwe-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blamey_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-blamey-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fop_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-fop-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foc_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-foc-12">[12]</a></sup> Roots are simple in structure, with a single primary root that grows vertically downward, later producing smaller lateral roots. These roots form interactions with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhizosphere_(ecology)" title="Rhizosphere (ecology)">rhizosphere</a> bacteria such as <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_megaterium" title="Bacillus megaterium">Bacillus megaterium</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_(Arabidopsis_thaliana)_lehekarv_(trihhoom)_311_0804.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_%28Arabidopsis_thaliana%29_lehekarv_%28trihhoom%29_311_0804.JPG/170px-M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_%28Arabidopsis_thaliana%29_lehekarv_%28trihhoom%29_311_0804.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_%28Arabidopsis_thaliana%29_lehekarv_%28trihhoom%29_311_0804.JPG/255px-M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_%28Arabidopsis_thaliana%29_lehekarv_%28trihhoom%29_311_0804.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_%28Arabidopsis_thaliana%29_lehekarv_%28trihhoom%29_311_0804.JPG/340px-M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_%28Arabidopsis_thaliana%29_lehekarv_%28trihhoom%29_311_0804.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%BC%C3%BCrlooga_(Arabidopsis_thaliana)_lehekarv_(trihhoom)_311_0804.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscope" title="Scanning electron microscope">Scanning electron micrograph</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Trichome" title="Trichome">trichome</a>, a leaf hair of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, a unique structure that is made of a single cell</div></div></div>\n<p><i>A. thaliana</i> can complete its entire lifecycle in six weeks. The central stem that produces flowers grows after about three weeks, and the flowers naturally self-pollinate. In the lab, <i>A. thaliana</i> may be grown in Petri plates, pots, or hydroponics, under fluorescent lights or in a greenhouse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The plant was first described in 1577 in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harz_Mountains" title="Harz Mountains">Harz Mountains</a> by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Johannes_Thal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Johannes Thal (page does not exist)">Johannes Thal</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;[<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johannes_Thal" title="de:Johannes Thal">de</a>]</span> (1542&#8211;1583), a physician from <a href="/wiki/Nordhausen" title="Nordhausen">Nordhausen</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Th%C3%BCringen" title="Th&#252;ringen">Th&#252;ringen</a>, Germany, who called it <i>Pilosella siliquosa</i>. In 1753, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> renamed the plant <i>Arabis thaliana</i> in honor of Thal. In 1842, the German botanist <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Heynhold" title="Gustav Heynhold">Gustav Heynhold</a> erected the new genus <i>Arabidopsis</i> and placed the plant in that genus. The genus name, <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Arabidopsis" title="wikt:Arabidopsis">Arabidopsis</a></i>, comes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_(language)" title="Greek (language)">Greek</a>, meaning "resembling <i><a href="/wiki/Arabis" title="Arabis">Arabis</a></i>" (the genus in which Linnaeus had initially placed it).\n</p><p>Thousands of natural inbred accessions of <i>A. thaliana</i> have been collected from throughout its natural and introduced range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GenomesConsortium2016_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-GenomesConsortium2016-15">[15]</a></sup> These accessions exhibit considerable genetic and phenotypic variation which can be used to study the adaptation of this species to different environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GenomesConsortium2016_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-GenomesConsortium2016-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><i>A. thaliana</i> is native to <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, and human observations indicate its geographic distribution is rather continuous from the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> to <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> to <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> It also appears to be native in tropical alpine ecosystems in Africa and perhaps South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> It has been introduced and naturalized worldwide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eol_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-eol-19">[19]</a></sup> including in North America ca. the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>A. thaliana</i> readily grows and often pioneers rocky, sandy and calcareous soils. It is generally considered a weed, due to its widespread distribution in agricultural fields, roadside, railway lines, waste ground and other disturbed habitat,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eol_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-eol-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> but due to its limited competitive ability and small size it is not categorized as a noxious weed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Like most Brassicaceae species, <i>A. thaliana</i> is edible by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humans" title="Humans">humans</a> as a salad or cooked, but it does not enjoy a widespread use as a spring vegetable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eol_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-eol-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_as_a_model_organism">Use as a model organism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Use as a model organism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_research_on_Arabidopsis_thaliana" title="History of research on Arabidopsis thaliana">History of research on Arabidopsis thaliana</a></div>\n<p>Botanists and biologists began to research <i>A. thaliana</i> in the early 1900s, and the first systematic description of mutants was done around 1945.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> <i>A. thaliana</i> is now widely used for studying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_sciences" title="Plant sciences">plant sciences</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>, population genetics, and plant development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Although <i>A. thaliana</i> has little direct significance for agriculture, it has several traits that make it a useful model for understanding the genetic, cellular, and molecular biology of flowering plants.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arabidopsis_mutants.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="675" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Arabidopsis_mutants.jpg/220px-Arabidopsis_mutants.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Arabidopsis_mutants.jpg/330px-Arabidopsis_mutants.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Arabidopsis_mutants.jpg/440px-Arabidopsis_mutants.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arabidopsis_mutants.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A double flower mutant of arabidopsis, first documented in 1873</div></div></div>\n<p>The first mutant in <i>A. thaliana</i> was documented in 1873 by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Braun" title="Alexander Braun">Alexander Braun</a>, describing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_flower" title="Double flower">double flower</a> phenotype (the mutated gene was likely <i>Agamous</i>, cloned and characterized in 1990).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> However, not until 1943 did <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Laibach" title="Friedrich Laibach">Friedrich Laibach</a> (who had published the chromosome number in 1907) propose <i>A. thaliana</i> as a model organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyerowitz2001_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-meyerowitz2001-28">[28]</a></sup> His student, Erna Reinholz, published her thesis on <i>A. thaliana</i> in 1945, describing the first collection of <i>A. thaliana</i> mutants that they generated using <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> <a href="/wiki/Mutagenesis" title="Mutagenesis">mutagenesis</a>. Laibach continued his important contributions to <i>A. thaliana</i> research by collecting a large number of accessions (often questionably referred to as \'<a href="/wiki/Ecotype" title="Ecotype">ecotypes</a>\'). With the help of Albert Kranz, these were organised into a large collection of 750 natural accessions of <i>A. thaliana</i> from around the world.\n</p><p>In the 1950s and 1960s, John Langridge and <a href="/wiki/George_R%C3%A9dei" title="George R&#233;dei">George R&#233;dei</a> played an important role in establishing <i>A. thaliana</i> as a useful organism for biological laboratory experiments. R&#233;dei wrote several scholarly reviews instrumental in introducing the model to the scientific community. The start of the <i>A. thaliana</i> research community dates to a newsletter called <i>Arabidopsis</i> Information Service (AIS), established in 1964. The first International <i>Arabidopsis</i> Conference was held in 1965, in <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen" title="G&#246;ttingen">G&#246;ttingen</a>, Germany.\n</p><p>In the 1980s, <i>A. thaliana</i> started to become widely used in plant research laboratories around the world. It was one of several candidates that included <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petunia" title="Petunia">petunia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyerowitz2001_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-meyerowitz2001-28">[28]</a></sup> The latter two were attractive, since they were easily transformable with the then-current technologies, while maize was a well-established genetic model for plant biology. 1986 was a breakthrough year for <i>A. thaliana</i> as a model plant, in which <a href="/wiki/Transfer_DNA" title="Transfer DNA">T-DNA</a>-mediated <a href="/wiki/Transformation_(genetics)#Plants" title="Transformation (genetics)">transformation</a> and the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clone_(genetics)" title="Clone (genetics)">cloned</a> <i>A. thaliana</i> gene were described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genomics">Genomics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Genomics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plastomap_of_Arabidopsis_thaliana.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="483" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Plastomap_of_Arabidopsis_thaliana.svg/220px-Plastomap_of_Arabidopsis_thaliana.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Plastomap_of_Arabidopsis_thaliana.svg/330px-Plastomap_of_Arabidopsis_thaliana.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Plastomap_of_Arabidopsis_thaliana.svg/440px-Plastomap_of_Arabidopsis_thaliana.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plastomap_of_Arabidopsis_thaliana.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chloroplast genome map of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_NC_000932_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI_NC_000932-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sato1999_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sato1999-32">[32]</a></sup> Introns are in grey. Some genes consist of 5&#8242; and 3&#8242; portions. Strand 1 and 2 genes are transcribed clockwise and counterclockwise, respectively. The innermost circle provides the boundaries of the large and small single copy regions (LSC and SSC, violet) separated by a pair of inverted repeats (IRa and IRb, black).</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_genome">Nuclear genome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Nuclear genome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The small size of its <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a>, and the fact that it is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">diploid</a>, makes <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> useful for genetic mapping and <a href="/wiki/Sequencing" title="Sequencing">sequencing</a> &#8212; with about 135 <a href="/wiki/Base_pair#Length_measurements" title="Base pair">mega base pairs</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> and five <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a>, <i>A. thaliana</i> has one of the smallest genomes among plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genome_Assembly_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Genome_Assembly-8">[8]</a></sup> It was long thought to have the smallest genome of all flowering plants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> but that title is now considered to belong to plants in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Genlisea" title="Genlisea">Genlisea</a></i>, order <a href="/wiki/Lamiales" title="Lamiales">Lamiales</a>, with <i><a href="/wiki/Genlisea_tuberosa" title="Genlisea tuberosa">Genlisea tuberosa</a></i>, a carnivorous plant, showing a genome size of approximately 61&#160;Mbp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fleischmann_2014_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fleischmann_2014-35">[35]</a></sup> It was the first plant genome to be sequenced, completed in 2000 by the Arabidopsis Genome Initiative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The most up-to-date version of the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome is maintained by the Arabidopsis Information Resource (TAIR).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Much work has been done to assign functions to its 27,000 <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> and the 35,000 proteins they encode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Post-genomic research, such as metabolomics, has also provided useful insights to the metabolism of this species and how environmental perturbations <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> can affect metabolic processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chloroplast_genome">Chloroplast genome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Chloroplast genome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The plastome of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> is a 154,478 base pair long DNA molecule,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_NC_000932_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI_NC_000932-31">[31]</a></sup> a size typically encountered in most flowering plants (see the <a href="/wiki/List_of_sequenced_plastomes#Flowering_plants" title="List of sequenced plastomes">list of sequenced plastomes</a>). It comprises 136 genes coding for small subunit ribosomal proteins (<i>rps</i>, in yellow: see figure), large subunit ribosomal proteins (<i>rpl</i>, orange), hypothetical chloroplast open reading frame proteins (<i>ycf</i>, lemon), proteins involved in photosynthetic reactions (green) or in other functions (red), ribosomal RNAs (<i>rrn</i>, blue), and transfer RNAs (<i>trn</i>, black).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sato1999_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sato1999-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Transformation_(genetics)" title="Transformation (genetics)">Genetic transformation</a> of <i>A. thaliana</i> is routine, utilizing <i><a href="/wiki/Agrobacterium_tumefaciens" title="Agrobacterium tumefaciens">Agrobacterium tumefaciens</a></i> to transfer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deoxyribonucleic_acid" title="Deoxyribonucleic acid">DNA</a> into the plant genome. The current protocol, termed "floral dip", involves simply dipping flowers into a solution containing <i>Agrobacterium</i> carrying a plasmid of interest and a detergent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> This method avoids the need for <a href="/wiki/Tissue_culture" title="Tissue culture">tissue culture</a> or plant regeneration.\n</p><p>The <i>A. thaliana</i> gene knockout collections are a unique resource for plant biology made possible by the availability of high-throughput transformation and funding for genomics resources. The site of T-DNA insertions has been determined for over 300,000 independent transgenic lines, with the information and seeds accessible through online <a class="external text" href="http://signal.salk.edu/cgi-bin/tdnaexpress" rel="nofollow">T-DNA databases</a>. Through these collections, insertional mutants are available for most genes in <i>A. thaliana</i>.\n</p><p>Characterized accessions and mutant lines of <i>A. thaliana</i> serve as experimental material in laboratory studies. The most commonly used background lines are L<i>er</i> (Landsberg <i>erecta</i>), and Col, or Columbia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arabidopsis.info_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-arabidopsis.info-43">[43]</a></sup> Other background lines less-often cited in the scientific literature are Ws, or Wassilewskija, C24, Cvi, or Cape Verde Islands, Nossen, etc. (see for ex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>) Sets of closely related accessions named Col-0, Col-1, etc., have been obtained and characterized; in general, mutant lines are available through stock centers, of which best-known are the Nottingham Arabidopsis Stock Center-NASC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arabidopsis.info_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-arabidopsis.info-43">[43]</a></sup> and the Arabidopsis Biological Resource Center-ABRC in Ohio, USA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\nThe Col-0 accession was selected by R&#233;dei from within a (nonirradiated) population of seeds designated \'Landsberg\' which he received from Laibach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Columbia (named for the location of R&#233;dei\'s former institution, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Missouri" title="University of Missouri">University of Missouri</a> in <a href="/wiki/Columbia,_Missouri" title="Columbia, Missouri">Columbia</a>) was the reference accession sequenced in the <i>Arabidopsis</i> Genome Initiative. The L<i>er</i> (Landsberg <i>erecta</i>) line was selected by R&#233;dei (because of its short stature) from a Landsberg population he had mutagenized with X-rays. As the L<i>er</i> collection of mutants is derived from this initial line, L<i>er</i>-0 does not correspond to the Landsberg accessions, which designated La-0, La-1, etc.\n</p><p>Trichome formation is initiated by the GLABROUS1 protein. <a href="/wiki/Gene_knockout" title="Gene knockout">Knockouts</a> of the corresponding gene lead to glabrous plants. This <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a> has already been used in <a href="/wiki/Genome_editing" title="Genome editing">gene editing</a> experiments and might be of interest as visual marker for plant research to improve gene editing methods such as <a href="/wiki/CRISPR#Genome_engineering" title="CRISPR">CRISPR/Cas9.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-Mendelian_inheritance_controversy">Non-Mendelian inheritance controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Non-Mendelian inheritance controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 2005, scientists at <a href="/wiki/Purdue_University" title="Purdue University">Purdue University</a> proposed that <i>A. thaliana</i> possessed an alternative to previously known mechanisms of <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">DNA repair</a>, producing an unusual pattern of <a href="/wiki/Mendelian_inheritance" title="Mendelian inheritance">inheritance</a>. However, the phenomenon observed (reversion of mutant copies of the <i><a href="/wiki/HOTHEAD_(gene)" title="HOTHEAD (gene)">HOTHEAD</a></i> gene to a wild-type state) was later suggested to be an artifact because the mutants show increased outcrossing due to organ fusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_cycle">Life cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Life cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The plant\'s small size and rapid lifecycle are also advantageous for research. Having specialized as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spring_ephemeral" title="Spring ephemeral">spring ephemeral</a>, it has been used to found several laboratory strains that take about six weeks from germination to mature seed. The small size of the plant is convenient for cultivation in a small space, and it produces many seeds. Further, the selfing nature of this plant assists genetic experiments. Also, as an individual plant can produce several thousand seeds; each of the above criteria leads to <i>A. thaliana</i> being valued as a genetic model organism.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flower_development">Flower development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Flower development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/ABC_model_of_flower_development" title="ABC model of flower development">ABC model of flower development</a></div>\n<p><i>A. thaliana </i>has been extensively studied as a model for flower development. The developing flower has four basic organs: <a href="/wiki/Sepal" title="Sepal">sepals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petal" title="Petal">petals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stamen" title="Stamen">stamens</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gynoecium" title="Gynoecium">carpels</a> (which go on to form <a href="/wiki/Gynoecium" title="Gynoecium">pistils</a>). These organs are arranged in a series of whorls: four sepals on the outer whorl, followed by four petals inside this, six stamens, and a central carpel region. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeotic" title="Homeotic">Homeotic</a> mutations in <i>A. thaliana</i> result in the change of one organ to another &#8212; in the case of the <i>agamous</i> mutation, for example, stamens become petals and carpels are replaced with a new flower, resulting in a recursively repeated sepal-petal-petal pattern.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ABC_flower_development.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="363" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ABC_flower_development.svg/150px-ABC_flower_development.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ABC_flower_development.svg/225px-ABC_flower_development.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ABC_flower_development.svg/300px-ABC_flower_development.svg.png 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ABC_flower_development.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The ABC model of flower development was developed through studying <i>A. thaliana</i>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Observations of homeotic mutations led to the formulation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABC_model" title="ABC model">ABC model of flower development</a> by <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Coen" title="Enrico Coen">E. Coen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elliot_Meyerowitz" title="Elliot Meyerowitz">E. Meyerowitz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> According to this model, floral organ identity genes are divided into three classes: class A genes (which affect sepals and petals), class B genes (which affect petals and stamens), and class C genes (which affect stamens and carpels). These genes code for <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factors</a> that combine to cause tissue specification in their respective regions during development. Although developed through study of <i>A. thaliana</i> flowers, this model is generally applicable to other <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaf_development">Leaf development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Leaf development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Studies of <i>A. thaliana</i> have provided considerable insights with regards to the genetics of leaf morphogenesis, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Dicotyledon" title="Dicotyledon">dicotyledon-type</a> plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Much of the understanding has come from analyzing mutants in leaf development, some of which were identified in the 1960s, but were not analysed with genetic and molecular techniques until the mid-1990s. <i>A. thaliana</i> leaves are well suited to studies of leaf development because they are relatively simple and stable.\n</p><p>Using <i>A. thaliana</i>, the genetics behind leaf shape development have become more clear and have been broken down into three stages: The initiation of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Leaf_primordium&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Leaf primordium (page does not exist)">leaf primordium</a>, the establishment of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dorsiventrality&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dorsiventrality (page does not exist)">dorsiventrality</a>, and the development of a marginal <a href="/wiki/Meristem" title="Meristem">meristem</a>. Leaf primordia are initiated by the suppression of the genes and proteins of the class I <i><a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_plants" title="Evolutionary history of plants">KNOX</a></i> family (such as <i>SHOOT APICAL MERISTEMLESS</i>). These class I KNOX proteins directly suppress <a href="/wiki/Gibberellin" title="Gibberellin">gibberellin</a> biosynthesis in the leaf primordium. Many genetic factors were found to be involved in the suppression of these class I <i>KNOX</i> genes in leaf primordia (such as <i>ASYMMETRIC LEAVES1,</i> <i>BLADE-ON-PETIOLE1</i>, <i>SAWTOOTH1</i>, etc.). Thus, with this suppression, the levels of gibberellin increase and leaf primordium initiates growth.\n</p><p>The establishment of leaf dorsiventrality is important since the <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location" title="Anatomical terms of location">dorsal</a> (adaxial) surface of the leaf is different from the ventral (abaxial) surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microscopy">Microscopy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Microscopy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>A. thaliana</i> is well suited for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_microscopy" title="Light microscopy">light microscopy</a> analysis. Young <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seedlings" title="Seedlings">seedlings</a> on the whole, and their roots in particular, are relatively translucent. This, together with their small size, facilitates live cell imaging using both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorescence_microscopy" title="Fluorescence microscopy">fluorescence</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confocal_laser_scanning_microscopy" title="Confocal laser scanning microscopy">confocal laser scanning microscopy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> By wet-mounting seedlings in water or in culture media, plants may be imaged uninvasively, obviating the need for <a href="/wiki/Fixation_(histology)" title="Fixation (histology)">fixation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dissection" title="Dissection">sectioning</a> and allowing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time-lapse" title="Time-lapse">time-lapse</a> measurements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Fluorescent protein constructs can be introduced through <a href="/wiki/Transformation_(genetics)" title="Transformation (genetics)">transformation</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Plant_morphology" title="Plant morphology">developmental</a> stage of each cell can be inferred from its location in the plant or by using <a href="/wiki/Green_fluorescent_protein" title="Green fluorescent protein">fluorescent protein</a> <a href="/wiki/Biomarker" title="Biomarker">markers</a>, allowing detailed <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">developmental analysis</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Light_sensing.2C_light_emission.2C_and_circadian_biology"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Light_sensing,_light_emission,_and_circadian_biology">Light sensing, light emission, and circadian biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Light sensing, light emission, and circadian biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The photoreceptors <a href="/wiki/Phytochrome" title="Phytochrome">phytochromes</a> A, B, C, D, and E mediate red light-based phototropic response. Understanding the function of these receptors has helped plant biologists understand the signalling cascades that regulate <a href="/wiki/Photoperiodism" title="Photoperiodism">photoperiodism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germination" title="Germination">germination</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De-etiolation" title="De-etiolation">de-etiolation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shade_avoidance" title="Shade avoidance">shade avoidance</a> in plants.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/UVR8" title="UVR8">UVR8</a> protein detects <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV-B" title="UV-B">UV-B</a> light and mediates response to this DNA damaging wavelength.\n</p><p><i>A. thaliana</i> was used extensively in the study of the genetic basis of <a href="/wiki/Phototropism" title="Phototropism">phototropism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplast</a> alignment, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomata" title="Stomata">stomatal</a> aperture and other blue light-influenced processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> These traits respond to blue light, which is perceived by the <a href="/wiki/Phototropin" title="Phototropin">phototropin</a> light receptors. Arabidopsis has also been important in understanding the functions of another blue light receptor, <a href="/wiki/Cryptochrome" title="Cryptochrome">cryptochrome</a>, which is especially important for light entrainment to control the plants\' <a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm" title="Circadian rhythm">circadian rhythms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> When the onset of darkness is unusually early, <i>A. thaliana</i> reduces its metabolism of starch by an amount that effectively requires <a href="/wiki/Plant_arithmetic" title="Plant arithmetic">division</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Light responses were even found in roots, previously thought to be largely insensitive to light. While the <a href="/wiki/Gravitropism" title="Gravitropism">gravitropic</a> response of <i>A. thaliana</i> root organs is their predominant tropic response, specimens treated with <a href="/wiki/Mutagen" title="Mutagen">mutagens</a> and selected for the absence of gravitropic action showed negative phototropic response to blue or white light, and positive response to red light, indicating that the roots also show positive phototropism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2000, Dr. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Janet_Braam&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Janet Braam (page does not exist)">Janet Braam</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rice_University" title="Rice University">Rice University</a> genetically engineered <i>A. thaliana</i> to glow in the dark when touched. The effect was visible to ultrasensitive cameras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Multiple efforts, including the <a href="/wiki/Glowing_Plant_project" title="Glowing Plant project">Glowing Plant project</a>, have sought to use <i>A. thaliana</i> to increase plant luminescence intensity towards commercially viable levels.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_Moon">On the Moon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: On the Moon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As of January 2, 2019, China\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chang%27e-4" title="Chang\'e-4">Chang\'e-4</a> lander brought <i>A. thaliana</i> to the moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letzter2019_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Letzter2019-63">[63]</a></sup> A small <a href="/wiki/Microcosm_(experimental_ecosystem)" title="Microcosm (experimental ecosystem)">microcosm</a> \'tin\' in the lander contained <i>A. thaliana</i>, seeds of <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Bombyx_mori" title="Bombyx mori">silkworm</a> eggs. As plants would support the silkworms with oxygen, and the silkworms would in turn provide the plants with necessary carbon dioxide and nutrients through their waste,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> researchers will evaluate whether plants successfully perform <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, and grow and bloom in the lunar environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letzter2019_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Letzter2019-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Plant.E2.80.93pathogen_interactions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Plant&#8211;pathogen_interactions">Plant&#8211;pathogen interactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Plant&#8211;pathogen interactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>It is important to understand how plants achieve resistance to protect the world\'s food production, as well as the agriculture industry. Many model systems have been developed to better understand interactions between plants and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacterial" title="Bacterial">bacterial</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oomycete" title="Oomycete">oomycete</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viral</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematode</a> pathogens. <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> has been a powerful tool for the study of the subdicipline of <a href="/wiki/Plant_pathology" title="Plant pathology">plant pathology</a>, that is, the interaction between plants and disease-causing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pathogens" title="Pathogens">pathogens</a>.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Pathogen type</th>\n<th>Example in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Bacteria</b></td>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_syringae" title="Pseudomonas syringae">Pseudomonas syringae</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Xanthomonas_campestris_pv._campestris" title="Xanthomonas campestris pv. campestris"><i>Xanthomonas campestris</i></a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Fungi</b></td>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/Colletotrichum_destructivum" title="Colletotrichum destructivum">Colletotrichum destructivum</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Botrytis_cinerea" title="Botrytis cinerea">Botrytis cinerea</a></i>, <i>Golovinomyces orontii</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Oomycete</b></td>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/Hyaloperonospora_arabidopsidis" title="Hyaloperonospora arabidopsidis">Hyaloperonospora arabidopsidis</a></i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Viral</b></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Cauliflower_mosaic_virus" title="Cauliflower mosaic virus">Cauliflower mosaic virus (CaMV)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_mosaic_virus" title="Tobacco mosaic virus">tomato mosaic virus (TMV)</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Nematode</b></td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meloidogyne" title="Meloidogyne"><i>Meloidogyne incognita</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Heterodera" title="Heterodera"><i>Heterodera schachtii</i></a>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The use of <i>A. thaliana</i> has led to many breakthroughs in the advancement of knowledge of how plants manifest <a href="/wiki/Plant_disease_resistance" title="Plant disease resistance">plant disease resistance</a>. The reason most plants are resistant to most pathogens is through nonhost resistance. This is, not all pathogens will infect all plants. An example where <i>A. thaliana</i> was used to determine the genes responsible for nonhost resistance is <i><a href="/wiki/Blumeria_graminis" title="Blumeria graminis">Blumeria graminis</a></i>, the causal agent of powdery mildew of grasses. <i>A. thaliana</i> mutants were developed using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutagenic" title="Mutagenic">mutagen</a> <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_methanesulfonate" title="Ethyl methanesulfonate">ethyl methanesulfonate</a> and screened to identify mutants with increased infection by <i>B. graminis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The mutants with higher infection rates are referred to as PEN mutants due to the ability of <i>B. graminis</i> to penetrate <i>A. thaliana</i> to begin the disease process. The PEN genes were later mapped to identify the genes responsible for nonhost resistance to <i>B. graminis</i>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ArabidopsisPlantPathology.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="334" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="334" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/68/ArabidopsisPlantPathology.jpg" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ArabidopsisPlantPathology.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>Components of pathogen recognition in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> </b><br/>A schematic of PAPM-triggered immunity, to be specific recognition of flagellin by FLS2 (top left), effector-triggered immunity depicted through the recognition of avrRpt2 by RPS2 through RIN4 (top-right), microscopic view of callose deposition in an <i>A. thaliana</i> leaf (bottom left), an example of no hypersensitive response (HR), top, and HR in <i>A. thaliana</i> leaves (bottom right)</div></div></div>\n<p>In general, when a plant is exposed to a pathogen, or <a href="/wiki/Commensalism" title="Commensalism">nonpathogenic</a> microbe, there is an initial response, known as PAMP-triggered immunity (PTI), because the plant detects conserved motifs known as <a href="/wiki/Pathogen-associated_molecular_pattern" title="Pathogen-associated molecular pattern">pathogen-associated molecular patterns</a> (PAMPs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> These PAMPs are detected by specialized <a href="/wiki/Receptor_(biochemistry)" title="Receptor (biochemistry)">receptors</a> in the host known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pattern_recognition_receptors" title="Pattern recognition receptors">pattern recognition receptors</a> (PRRs) on the plant cell surface.\n</p><p>The best-characterized PRR in <i>A. thaliana</i> is FLS2 (Flagellin-Sensing2), which recognizes bacterial <a href="/wiki/Flagellin" title="Flagellin">flagellin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> a specialized organelle used by microorganisms for the purpose of motility, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Ligand_(biochemistry)" title="Ligand (biochemistry)">ligand</a> flg22, which comprises the 22 amino acids recognized by FLS2. Discovery of FLS2 was facilitated by the identification of an <i>A. thaliana</i> ecotype, Ws-0, that was unable to detect flg22, leading to the identification of the gene encoding FLS2. <a href="/wiki/Pamela_Ronald#Xa21:_Pattern_recognition_receptor-mediated_immunity" title="Pamela Ronald">FLS2 shows striking similarity to rice XA21, the first PRR isolated in 1995</a>\n</p><p>A second PRR, EF-Tu receptor (EFR), identified in <i>A. thaliana</i>, recognizes the bacterial <a href="/wiki/EF-Tu" title="EF-Tu">EF-Tu</a> protein, the prokaryotic elongation factor used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protein_synthesis" title="Protein synthesis">protein synthesis</a>, as well as the laboratory-used ligand elf18.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Using <a href="/wiki/Agrobacterium#Uses_in_biotechnology" title="Agrobacterium"><i>Agrobacterium</i>-mediated transformation</a>, a technique that takes advantage of the natural process by which <i>Agrobacterium</i> transfers genes into host plants, the EFR gene was transformed into <i><a href="/wiki/Nicotiana_benthamiana" title="Nicotiana benthamiana">Nicotiana benthamiana</a></i>, tobacco plant that does not recognize EF-Tu, thereby permitting recognition of bacterial EF-Tu<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> thereby confirming EFR as the receptor of EF-Tu.\n</p><p>Both FLS2 and EFR use similar <a href="/wiki/Signal_transduction" title="Signal transduction">signal transduction</a> pathways to initiate PTI. <i>A. thaliana</i> has been instrumental in dissecting these pathways to better understand the regulation of immune responses, the most notable one being the <a href="/wiki/Mitogen-activated_protein_kinase" title="Mitogen-activated protein kinase">mitogen-activated protein kinase</a> (MAP kinase) cascade. Downstream responses of PTI include <a href="/wiki/Callose" title="Callose">callose</a> deposition, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidative_burst" title="Oxidative burst">oxidative burst</a>, and transcription of defense-related genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>PTI is able to combat pathogens in a nonspecific manner. A stronger and more specific response in plants is that of effector-triggered immunity (ETI). ETI is dependent upon the recognition of pathogen effectors, proteins secreted by the pathogen that alter functions in the host, by plant <a href="/wiki/R_gene" title="R gene">resistance genes (R-genes)</a>, often described as <a href="/wiki/Gene-for-gene_relationship" title="Gene-for-gene relationship">a gene-for-gene relationship</a>. This recognition may occur directly or indirectly via a guardee protein in a hypothesis known as <a href="/wiki/Gene-for-gene_relationship#The_guard_hypothesis" title="Gene-for-gene relationship">the guard hypothesis</a>. The first R-gene cloned in <i>A. thaliana</i> was RPS2 (resistance to <i>Pseudomonas syringe</i> 2), which is responsible for recognition of the effector avrRpt2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The bacterial effector avrRpt2 is delivered into <i>A. thaliana</i> via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_III_secretion_system" title="Type III secretion system">Type III secretion system</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_syringae#Pseudomonas_syringae_as_a_model_system" title="Pseudomonas syringae"><i>P. syringae pv tomato</i> strain DC3000</a>. Recognition of avrRpt2 by RPS2 occurs via the guardee protein RIN4, which is cleaved . Recognition of a pathogen effector leads to a dramatic immune response known as the <a href="/wiki/Hypersensitive_response" title="Hypersensitive response">hypersensitive response</a>, in which the infected plant cells undergo cell death to prevent the spread of the pathogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Systemic_acquired_resistance" title="Systemic acquired resistance">Systemic acquired resistance</a> (SAR) is another example of resistance that is better understood in plants because of research done in <i>A. thaliana</i>. Benzothiadiazol (BTH), a <a href="/wiki/Salicylic_acid" title="Salicylic acid">salicylic acid</a> (SA) analog, has been used historically as an antifungal compound in crop plants. BTH, as well as SA, has been shown to induce SAR in plants. The initiation of the SAR pathway was first demonstrated in <i>A. thaliana</i> in which increased SA levels are recognized by nonexpresser of PR genes 1 (NPR1)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> due to redox change in the cytosol, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduction</a> of NPR1. NPR1, which usually exists in a multiplex (oligomeric) state, becomes monomeric (a single unit) upon reduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> When NPR1 becomes monomeric, it <a href="/wiki/Protein_targeting#Protein_translocation" title="Protein targeting">translocates</a> to the nucleus, where it interacts with many TGA <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factors</a>, and is able to induce pathogen-related genes such as PR1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Another example of SAR would be the research done with transgenic tobacco plants, which express bacterial salicylate hydroxylase, nahG gene, requires the accumulation of SA for its expression <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delaney,_T._1994_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Delaney,_T._1994-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_aspect_of_plant-pathogen_resistance">Evolutionary aspect of plant-pathogen resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Evolutionary aspect of plant-pathogen resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Plants are affected by multiple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pathogens" title="Pathogens">pathogens</a> throughout their lifetime. In response to the presence of pathogens, plants have evolved receptors on the cell surface to detect and respond to pathogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bent1994_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bent1994-80">[80]</a></sup> <i>Arabidopsis Thaliana</i> is a model organism used to determine specific defense mechanisms of plant-pathogen resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zipfel,_C._2004_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zipfel,_C._2004-81">[81]</a></sup> These plants have special receptors on their cell surfaces that allow for detection of pathogens and initiate mechanisms to inhibit pathogen growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zipfel,_C._2004_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zipfel,_C._2004-81">[81]</a></sup> They contain two receptors, FLS2 (bacterial flagellin receptor) and EF-Tu (bacterial EF-Tu protein), which use signal transduction pathways to initiate the disease response pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zipfel,_C._2004_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zipfel,_C._2004-81">[81]</a></sup> The pathway leads to the recognition of the pathogen causing the infected cells to undergo cell death to stop the spread of the pathogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zipfel,_C._2004_81-3"><a href="#cite_note-Zipfel,_C._2004-81">[81]</a></sup> Plants with FLS2 and EF-Tu receptors have shown to have increased fitness in the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delaney,_T._1994_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Delaney,_T._1994-79">[79]</a></sup> This has led to the belief that plant-pathogen resistance is an evolutionary mechanism that has built up over generations to respond to dynamic environments, such as increased predation and extreme temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delaney,_T._1994_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-Delaney,_T._1994-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>A. thaliana</i> has also been used to study <a href="/wiki/Systemic_acquired_resistance" title="Systemic acquired resistance">systemic acquired resistance</a> (SAR).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawton,_K._1996_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lawton,_K._1996-82">[82]</a></sup>\nThis pathway utilizes Benzothiadiazol, a chemical inducer, to induce transcription factors, mRNA, of SAR genes. This accumulation of transcription factors leads to inhibition of pathogen-related genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawton,_K._1996_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lawton,_K._1996-82">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Plant-pathogen interactions are important for understanding of how plants have evolved to combat different types of pathogens that may affect them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delaney,_T._1994_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-Delaney,_T._1994-79">[79]</a></sup> Variation in resistance of plants across populations is due to variation in environmental factors. Plants that have evolved resistance, whether it be the general variation or the SAR variation, have been able to live longer and hold off necrosis of their tissue (premature death of cells), which leads to better adaptation and fitness for populations that are in rapidly changing environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delaney,_T._1994_79-4"><a href="#cite_note-Delaney,_T._1994-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_research">Other research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Ongoing research on <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> is being performed on the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> by the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>. The goals are to study the growth and reproduction of plants from seed to seed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microgravity" title="Microgravity">microgravity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>\'Plant on a chip\' devices in which <i>A. thaliana</i> tissues can be cultured in semi in-vitro conditions have been described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Use of these devices may aid our understanding of pollen tube guidance and the mechanism of sexual reproduction in <i>A. thaliana.</i>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-pollination">Self-pollination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Self-pollination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>A. thaliana</i> is a predominantly self-pollinating plant with an outcrossing rate estimated at less than 0.3%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> An analysis of the genome-wide pattern of linkage disequilibrium suggested that self-pollination evolved roughly a million years ago or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17656687_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17656687-87">[87]</a></sup> Meioses that lead to self-pollination are unlikely to produce significant beneficial genetic variability. However, these meioses can provide the adaptive benefit of recombinational repair of DNA damages during formation of germ cells at each generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Such a benefit may have been sufficient to allow the long-term persistence of meioses even when followed by self-fertilization. A physical mechanism for self-pollination in <i>A. thaliana</i> is through pre-anthesis autogamy, such that fertilisation takes place largely before flower opening.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Databases_and_other_resources">Databases and other resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabidopsis_thaliana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Databases and other resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/The_Arabidopsis_Information_Resource" title="The Arabidopsis Information Resource">TAIR</a> and <a class="external text" href="http://arabidopsis.info/" rel="nofollow">NASC</a>: curated sources for diverse genetic and molecular biology information, links to gene expression <a class="external text" href="http://www.arabidopsis.org/portals/expression/microarray/microarrayDatasetsV2.jsp" rel="nofollow">databases</a> etc.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Arabidopsis_Biological_Resource_Center" title="Arabidopsis Biological Resource Center">Arabidopsis Biological Resource Center</a> (Seed and DNA stocks)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Arabidopsis_Stock_Centre" title="Nottingham Arabidopsis Stock Centre">Nottingham Arabidopsis Stock Centre</a> (Seed and DNA stocks)</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span responses to salinity</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
